	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Mixed martial arts</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the fighting styles that combine arts, see <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_martial_arts" title="Hybrid martial arts">hybrid martial arts</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"NHB" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/NHB_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="NHB (disambiguation)">NHB (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"MMA" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/MMA_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="MMA (disambiguation)">MMA (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn" style="padding-bottom:0.2em;">Mixed Martial Arts (MMA)</caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="UFC 131 Carwin vs. JDS.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg/220px-UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg/330px-UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg/440px-UFC_131_Carwin_vs._JDS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2406" data-file-height="1944" /></a><div><a href="/wiki/Junior_dos_Santos" title="Junior dos Santos">Junior dos Santos</a>, in white shorts, and <a href="/wiki/Shane_Carwin" title="Shane Carwin">Shane Carwin</a>, in black shorts, during an MMA fight at the main event of <a href="/wiki/UFC_131" title="UFC 131">UFC 131</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, on June 11, 2011.</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;">Highest <a href="/wiki/Sports_governing_body" title="Sports governing body">governing&#160;body</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/International_Mixed_Martial_Arts_Federation" title="International Mixed Martial Arts Federation">International Mixed Martial Arts Federation</a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#eee;">Characteristics</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;">Contact</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">Full contact</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;"><a href="/wiki/Mixed-sex_sports" title="Mixed-sex sports">Mixed gender</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">Yes, separate male and female events</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;">Type</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">Indoor or outdoor</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;">Venue</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">Cage, ring, sandpit</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#eee;">Presence</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;">Country or&#160;region</th><td style="line-height:1.3em;">Worldwide</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.2em;line-height:1.2em; padding-right:1.0em;"><a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic</a></th><td style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Association_of_IOC_Recognised_International_Sports_Federations" title="Association of IOC Recognised International Sports Federations">not recognized</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> (IOC)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Mixed Martial Arts</b> (<b>MMA</b>) is a full-contact <a href="/wiki/Combat_sport" title="Combat sport">combat sport</a> that allows <a href="/wiki/Strike_(attack)" title="Strike (attack)">striking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grappling" title="Grappling">grappling</a>, both standing and on the ground, using techniques from various combat sports and <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a>. The first documented use of the term <i>mixed martial arts</i> was in a review of <a href="/wiki/UFC_1" title="UFC 1">UFC 1</a> by television critic <a href="/wiki/Howard_Rosenberg" title="Howard Rosenberg">Howard Rosenberg</a> in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1993_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1993-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The term gained popularity when newfullcontact.com, then one of the largest websites covering the sport, hosted and republished the article.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The question of who actually coined the term is subject to debate.<sup id="cite_ref-KirikJenness_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KirikJenness-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early 20th century, various mixed-style contests took place throughout <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and in the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tigers</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, there was the sport of <a href="/wiki/Vale_Tudo" class="mw-redirect" title="Vale Tudo">Vale Tudo</a>, in which fighters from various styles fought with little to no rules. The <a href="/wiki/Gracie_family" title="Gracie family">Gracie family</a> was known to promote <i>Vale Tudo</i> matches as a way to promote their own <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Jiu-Jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu">Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu</a> style.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> An early high-profile mixed martial arts bout was <a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura_vs._H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie">Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie</a> in 1951, fought between <a href="/wiki/Judoka" class="mw-redirect" title="Judoka">judoka</a> <a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura" title="Masahiko Kimura">Masahiko Kimura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_jiu_jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian jiu jitsu">Brazilian jiu jitsu</a> founder <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Hélio Gracie">Hélio Gracie</a> in Brazil. In the West, the concept of combining elements of multiple martial arts was popularized by <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">Bruce Lee</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jeet_Kune_Do" title="Jeet Kune Do">Jeet Kune Do</a> during the late 1960s to early 1970s. A precursor to modern MMA was the 1976 <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki" title="Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki">Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki</a> bout, fought between boxer <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> and wrestler <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Inoki" title="Antonio Inoki">Antonio Inoki</a> in Japan, where it later inspired the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Pancrase" title="Pancrase">Pancrase</a> in 1993 and <a href="/wiki/Pride_Fighting_Championships" title="Pride Fighting Championships">Pride Fighting Championships</a> in 1997.
</p><p>In 1980, <a href="/wiki/CV_Productions,_Inc." title="CV Productions, Inc.">CV Productions, Inc.</a> created the first regulated MMA league in the United States, called <i>Tough Guy Contest</i>, which was later renamed <i>Battle of the Superfighters</i>. The company sanctioned ten tournaments in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>. However, in 1983 the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_Senate" title="Pennsylvania State Senate">Pennsylvania State Senate</a> passed a bill prohibiting the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-NashJohn_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NashJohn-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WernerSam_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WernerSam-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, the <a href="/wiki/Gracie_family" title="Gracie family">Gracie family</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Jiu-Jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu">Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu</a>, developed in Brazil from the 1920s, to the United States by founding the <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Fighting_Championship" title="Ultimate Fighting Championship">Ultimate Fighting Championship</a> (UFC) MMA promotion company. The company held an event with almost no rules, mostly due the influence of <a href="/wiki/Art_Davie" title="Art Davie">Art Davie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rorion_Gracie" title="Rorion Gracie">Rorion Gracie</a> attempting to replicate <a href="/wiki/Vale_Tudo" class="mw-redirect" title="Vale Tudo">Vale Tudo</a> fights that existed in Brazil,<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and would later implement a different set of rules (example: eliminating kicking a grounded opponent), which differed from other leagues which were more in favour of realistic fights.<sup id="cite_ref-MixedMartialArts.com_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MixedMartialArts.com-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Originally promoted as a competition to find the most effective <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a> for real unarmed combat, competitors from different fighting styles were pitted against one another in contests with relatively few rules.<sup id="cite_ref-slate_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slate-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Later, individual fighters incorporated multiple martial arts into their style. MMA promoters were pressured to adopt <a href="/wiki/Mixed_martial_arts_rules" title="Mixed martial arts rules">additional rules</a> to increase competitors' safety, to comply with sport regulations and to broaden mainstream acceptance of the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Following these changes, the sport has seen increased popularity with a <a href="/wiki/Pay-per-view" title="Pay-per-view">pay-per-view</a> business that rivals <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Professional_wrestling" title="Professional wrestling">professional wrestling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PPV_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PPV-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Women&#39;s_mixed_martial_arts"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Women's mixed martial arts</span></a>
<ul>

</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leitai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Leitai.jpg/220px-Leitai.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="367" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Leitai.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leitai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial artist</a> preparing to throw his opponent during a <a href="/wiki/Lei_tai" title="Lei tai">lei tai</a> contest in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xwres8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Xwres8.jpg/220px-Xwres8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Xwres8.jpg/330px-Xwres8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Xwres8.jpg/440px-Xwres8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="532" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xwres8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wrestlers_(sculpture)" title="Wrestlers (sculpture)"><i>The Pancrastinae</i></a>: A statue portraying the <a href="/wiki/Pancratium" class="mw-redirect" title="Pancratium">pancratium</a>, an event which took place in the Roman <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>. Even as late as the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, statues were put up in Rome and other cities to honor remarkable pankratiasts. This statue, now part of the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi</a> collection, is a Roman copy of a lost Greek original, circa 3rd century BC.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Greek_Pankratists_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Greek_Pankratists_2.jpg/220px-Greek_Pankratists_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a5/Greek_Pankratists_2.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="240" data-file-height="121" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Greek_Pankratists_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A scene of Ancient Greek pankratiasts fighting. Originally found on a Panathenaic amphora, Lamberg Collection.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, there was a sport called <a href="/wiki/Pankration" title="Pankration">pankration</a>, which featured a combination of grappling and striking skills similar to those found in modern MMA. Pankration was formed by a combination of the already established wrestling and boxing traditions and, in Olympic terms, first featured in the 33rd <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympiad</a> in 648 BC. All strikes and holds were allowed with the exception of biting and gouging, which were banned. The fighters, called pankratiasts, fought until someone could not continue or signaled submission by raising their index finger; there were no rounds.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> According to E. Norman Gardiner, "No branch of athletics was more popular than the pankration."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> From its origins in Ancient Greece, pankration was later passed on to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combat_sport" title="Combat sport">combat sport</a> appeared in the form of <a href="/wiki/Leitai" class="mw-redirect" title="Leitai">Leitai</a>, a no-holds-barred mixed combat sport that combined <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial arts</a>, boxing and wrestling. There is evidence of similar mixed combat sports in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_martial_arts" title="Indian martial arts">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-era_precursors">Modern-era precursors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern-era precursors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The mid-19th century saw the prominence of the new sport <a href="/wiki/Savate" title="Savate">savate</a> in the combat sports circle. French savate fighters wanted to test their techniques against the traditional combat styles of its time. In 1852, a contest was held in France between French <i>savateurs</i> and English <a href="/wiki/Bare-knuckle_boxing" title="Bare-knuckle boxing">bare-knuckle boxers</a> in which French fighter Rambaud alias <i>la Resistance</i> fought English fighter Dickinson and won using his kicks. However, the English team still won the four other match-ups during the contest.<sup id="cite_ref-MAW_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAW-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Contests occurred in the late 19th to mid-20th century between French Savateurs and other combat styles. Examples include a 1905 fight between French savateur George Dubois and a <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a> practitioner Re-nierand which resulted in the latter winning by submission, as well as the highly publicized 1957 fight between French savateur and <a href="/wiki/Professional_boxer" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional boxer">professional boxer</a> Jacques Cayron and a young Japanese <a href="/wiki/Karateka" class="mw-redirect" title="Karateka">karateka</a> named Mochizuki Hiroo which ended when Cayron knocked Hiroo out with a <a href="/wiki/Hook_(boxing)" title="Hook (boxing)">hook</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MAW_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAW-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>No-holds-barred fighting reportedly took place in the late 1880s when wrestlers representing style of <a href="/wiki/Catch_wrestling" title="Catch wrestling">Catch wrestling</a> and many others met in tournaments and music-hall challenge matches throughout Europe. In the US, the first major encounter between a boxer and a wrestler in modern times took place in 1887 when <a href="/wiki/John_L._Sullivan" title="John L. Sullivan">John L. Sullivan</a>, then heavyweight world boxing champion, entered the ring with his trainer, wrestling champion <a href="/wiki/William_Muldoon" title="William Muldoon">William Muldoon</a>, and was slammed to the mat in two minutes. The next publicized encounter occurred in the late 1890s when future heavyweight boxing champion <a href="/wiki/Bob_Fitzsimmons" title="Bob Fitzsimmons">Bob Fitzsimmons</a> took on European wrestling champion <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Roeber" title="Ernest Roeber">Ernest Roeber</a>. In September 1901, <a href="/wiki/Frank_%22Paddy%22_Slavin" title="Frank &quot;Paddy&quot; Slavin">Frank "Paddy" Slavin</a>, who had been a contender for Sullivan's boxing title, knocked out future world wrestling champion <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gotch" title="Frank Gotch">Frank Gotch</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dawson_City" title="Dawson City">Dawson City</a>, Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The judo-practitioner Ren-nierand, who gained fame after defeating George Dubois, would fight again in another similar contest, which he lost to Ukrainian Catch wrestler Ivan Poddubny.<sup id="cite_ref-MAW_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MAW-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another early example of mixed martial arts was <a href="/wiki/Bartitsu" title="Bartitsu">Bartitsu</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Edward_William_Barton-Wright" title="Edward William Barton-Wright">Edward William Barton-Wright</a> founded in London in 1899. Combining <a href="/wiki/Catch_wrestling" title="Catch wrestling">catch wrestling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Savate" title="Savate">savate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jujutsu" title="Jujutsu">jujutsu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canne_de_combat" title="Canne de combat">canne de combat</a> (French stick fighting), Bartitsu was the first martial art known to have combined Asian and European fighting styles,<sup id="cite_ref-bart_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bart-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and which saw MMA-style contests throughout England, pitting European Catch wrestlers and Japanese Judoka champions against representatives of various European wrestling styles.<sup id="cite_ref-bart_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bart-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_MMA">Contemporary MMA</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Contemporary MMA">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The history of modern MMA competition can be traced to mixed style contests throughout Europe, Japan, and the Pacific Rim during the early 1900s.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In Japan, these contests were known as <i>merikan</i>, from the Japanese slang for "American [fighting]". <i>Merikan</i> contests were fought under a variety of rules, including points decision, best of three throws or knockdowns, and victory via knockout or submission.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sambo_(martial_art)" title="Sambo (martial art)">Sambo</a>, a martial art and combat sport developed in Russia in the early 1920s, merged various forms of combat styles such as wrestling, judo and striking into one unique martial art.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The popularity of professional wrestling, which was contested under various <a href="/wiki/Catch_wrestling" title="Catch wrestling">catch wrestling</a> rules at the time, waned after <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, when the sport split into two genres: "<a href="/wiki/Shoot_(professional_wrestling)" title="Shoot (professional wrestling)">shoot</a>", in which the fighters actually competed, and "<a href="/wiki/Work_(professional_wrestling)" class="mw-redirect" title="Work (professional wrestling)">show</a>", which evolved into modern <a href="/wiki/Professional_wrestling" title="Professional wrestling">professional wrestling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-willoughby_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willoughby-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In 1936, heavyweight boxing contender <a href="/wiki/Kingfish_Levinsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingfish Levinsky">Kingfish Levinsky</a> and veteran Catch wrestler <a href="/wiki/Ray_Steele_(US_wrestler)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ray Steele (US wrestler)">Ray Steele</a> competed in a mixed match, which Steele won in 35 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-willoughby_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-willoughby-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kimura_kesa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Kimura_kesa.jpg/220px-Kimura_kesa.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Kimura_kesa.jpg/330px-Kimura_kesa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/Kimura_kesa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kimura_kesa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura_vs._H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie">Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie</a>, a 1951 bout between <a href="/wiki/Judoka" class="mw-redirect" title="Judoka">judoka</a> <a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura" title="Masahiko Kimura">Masahiko Kimura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_jiu_jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian jiu jitsu">Brazilian jiu jitsu</a> founder <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Hélio Gracie">Hélio Gracie</a> in Brazil, was an early high-profile mixed martial arts bout.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1951, a high-profile mixed martial arts bout was <a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura_vs._H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie">Masahiko Kimura vs. Hélio Gracie</a>, which was fought between <a href="/wiki/Judoka" class="mw-redirect" title="Judoka">judoka</a> <a href="/wiki/Masahiko_Kimura" title="Masahiko Kimura">Masahiko Kimura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_jiu_jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian jiu jitsu">Brazilian jiu jitsu</a> founder <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Hélio Gracie">Hélio Gracie</a> in Brazil. Kimura defeated Gracie using a <a href="/wiki/Kimura_lock" class="mw-redirect" title="Kimura lock">gyaku-ude-garami</a> armlock, which later became known as the "Kimura" in Brazilian jiu jitsu.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In 1963, a catch wrestler and judoka "<a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">Judo</a>" <a href="/wiki/Gene_Lebell" class="mw-redirect" title="Gene Lebell">Gene Lebell</a> fought professional boxer Milo Savage in a <a href="/wiki/Catch_wrestling" title="Catch wrestling">no-holds-barred</a> match. Lebell won by <a href="/wiki/Harai_Goshi" class="mw-redirect" title="Harai Goshi">Harai Goshi</a> to rear naked choke, leaving Savage unconscious. This was the first televised bout of mixed-style fighting in North America. The hometown crowd was so enraged that they began to boo and throw chairs at Lebell.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 12, 1963, three karatekas from <i>Oyama dojo</i> (<a href="/wiki/Kyokushin" title="Kyokushin">kyokushin</a> later) went to the Lumpinee Boxing Stadium in Thailand and fought against three Muay Thai fighters. The three kyokushin karate fighters were <a href="/wiki/Tadashi_Nakamura_(martial_artist)" title="Tadashi Nakamura (martial artist)">Tadashi Nakamura</a>, Kenji Kurosaki and Akio Fujihira (also known as Noboru Osawa), while the Muay Thai team of three had only one authentic Thai fighter.<sup id="cite_ref-8_Limbs_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8_Limbs-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Japan won 2–1: Tadashi Nakamura and Akio Fujihira both knocked out their opponents with punches while Kenji Kurosaki, who fought the Thai, was knocked out by elbows. The Japanese fighter who lost, Kenji Kurosaki, was a kyokushin instructor, rather than a contender, and that he had stood in as a substitute for the absent chosen fighter. In June of the same year, karateka and future kickboxer <a href="/wiki/Tadashi_Sawamura" title="Tadashi Sawamura">Tadashi Sawamura</a> faced top Thai fighter Samarn Sor Adisorn: Sawamura was knocked down sixteen times on his way to defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-8_Limbs_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8_Limbs-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Sawamura went on to incorporate what he learned in that fight in kickboxing tournaments.
</p><p>During the late 1960s to early 1970s, the concept of combining the elements of multiple martial arts was popularized in the West by <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">Bruce Lee</a> via his system of <a href="/wiki/Jeet_Kune_Do" title="Jeet Kune Do">Jeet Kune Do</a>. Lee believed that "the best fighter is not a Boxer, Karate or Judo man. The best fighter is someone who can adapt to any style, to be formless, to adopt an individual's own style and not following the system of styles." In 2004, UFC President <a href="/wiki/Dana_White" title="Dana White">Dana White</a> would call Lee the "father of mixed martial arts" stating: "If you look at the way <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Lee" title="Bruce Lee">Bruce Lee</a> trained, the way he fought, and many of the things he wrote, he said the perfect style was no style. You take a little something from everything. You take the good things from every different discipline, use what works, and you throw the rest away".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A contemporary of Bruce Lee, <a href="/wiki/Wing_Chun" title="Wing Chun">Wing Chun</a> practitioner <a href="/wiki/Wong_Shun_Leung" title="Wong Shun Leung">Wong Shun Leung</a>, gained prominence fighting in 60-100 illegal <i><a href="/wiki/Street_fighting" title="Street fighting">beimo</a></i> fights against other Chinese martial artists of various styles. Wong also fought and won against Western fighters of other combat styles, such as his match against a Russian boxer named Giko,<sup id="cite_ref-PetersonPeterboro_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PetersonPeterboro-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> his televised fight against a <a href="/wiki/Fencing" title="Fencing">fencer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-LamLeBlanc_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LamLeBlanc-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> and his well-documented fight against <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Kung-Fu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kung-Fu">Kung-Fu</a> master Wu Ming Jeet.<sup id="cite_ref-Longley_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Longley-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Wong combined boxing and <a href="/wiki/Kickboxing" title="Kickboxing">kickboxing</a> into his <a href="/wiki/Kung_fu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kung fu">kung fu</a>, as Bruce Lee did.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8d/Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki.jpg/220px-Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8d/Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="265" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki" title="Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki">Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki</a>, a 1976 bout in Japan where boxer <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> fought wrestler <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Inoki" title="Antonio Inoki">Antonio Inoki</a>, was an important precursor to MMA contests.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_vs._Antonio_Inoki" title="Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki">Muhammad Ali vs. Antonio Inoki</a> took place in Japan in 1976. The classic match-up between professional boxer and professional wrestler turned sour as each fighter refused to engage in the other's style, and after a 15-round stalemate it was declared a draw. <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali" title="Muhammad Ali">Muhammad Ali</a> sustained a substantial amount of damage to his legs, as <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Inoki" title="Antonio Inoki">Antonio Inoki</a> slide-kicked him continuously for the duration of the bout, causing him to be hospitalized for the next three days.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The fight played an important role in the history of mixed martial arts.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, the match inspired Inoki's students <a href="/wiki/Masakatsu_Funaki" title="Masakatsu Funaki">Masakatsu Funaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Minoru_Suzuki" title="Minoru Suzuki">Minoru Suzuki</a> to found <a href="/wiki/Pancrase" title="Pancrase">Pancrase</a> in 1993, which in turn inspired the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Pride_Fighting_Championships" title="Pride Fighting Championships">Pride Fighting Championships</a> in 1997. Pride was acquired by its rival Ultimate Fighting Championship in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A well-documented fight between <a href="/wiki/Golden_Gloves" title="Golden Gloves">Golden Gloves</a> boxing champion <a href="/wiki/Cus_D%27Amato#Joey_Hadley" title="Cus D&#39;Amato">Joey Hadley</a> and Arkansas Karate Champion David Valovich happened on June 22, 1976 at Memphis Blues Baseball Park. The bout had mixed rules: the <a href="/wiki/Karateka" class="mw-redirect" title="Karateka">karateka</a> was allowed to use his fists, feet and knees, while the boxer could only use his fists. Hadley won the fight via knockout on the first round.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1988 <a href="/wiki/Rick_Roufus" title="Rick Roufus">Rick Roufus</a> challenged <a href="/wiki/Changpuek_Kiatsongrit" title="Changpuek Kiatsongrit">Changpuek Kiatsongrit</a> to a non-title <a href="/wiki/Muay_Thai" title="Muay Thai">Muay Thai</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Kickboxing" title="Kickboxing">kickboxing</a> super fight. Rick Roufus was at the time an undefeated Kickboxer and held both the <a href="/wiki/Karate_International_Council_of_Kickboxing" title="Karate International Council of Kickboxing">KICK</a> Super Middleweight World title and the PKC Middleweight U.S title. Changpuek Kiatsongrit was finding it increasingly difficult to get fights in Thailand as his weight (70&#160;kg) was not typical for Thailand, where competitive bouts at tend to be at the lower weights. Roufus knocked Changpuek down twice with punches in the first round, breaking Changpuek's jaw, but lost by technical knockout in the fourth round due to the culmination of low kicks to the legs that he was unprepared for. This match was the first popular fight which showcased the power of such low kicks to a predominantly Western audience.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_sport">Modern sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Modern sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The movement that led to the creation of the Brazilian mixed martial arts scenes was rooted in <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Jiu-Jitsu" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu">Brazilian Jiu-Jitsu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Vale_tudo" title="Vale tudo">vale tudo</a> events in Brazil.
</p><p>Vale tudo began in the 1920s and became gained renown through its association with the "<a href="/wiki/Gracie_challenge" class="mw-redirect" title="Gracie challenge">Gracie challenge</a>", which was issued by <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Gracie" title="Carlos Gracie">Carlos Gracie</a> and <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9lio_Gracie" title="Hélio Gracie">Hélio Gracie</a> and upheld later by descendants of the <a href="/wiki/Gracie_family" title="Gracie family">Gracie family</a>. The "Gracie Challenges" were held in the garages and gyms of the Gracie family members. When the popularity grew, these types of mixed bouts were a staple attraction at the carnivals in Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Early mixed-match martial arts <a href="/wiki/Professional_wrestling" title="Professional wrestling">professional wrestling</a> bouts in Japan (known as <i>Ishu Kakutōgi Sen</i> (異種格闘技戦), literally "heterogeneous combat sports bouts") became popular with <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Inoki" title="Antonio Inoki">Antonio Inoki</a> only in the 1970s. Inoki was a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Rikid%C5%8Dzan" title="Rikidōzan">Rikidōzan</a>, but also of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Gotch" title="Karl Gotch">Karl Gotch</a>, who trained numerous Japanese wrestlers in <a href="/wiki/Catch_wrestling" title="Catch wrestling">catch wrestling</a>.
</p><p>Regulated mixed martial arts competitions were first introduced in the United States by <a href="/wiki/CV_Productions,_Inc." title="CV Productions, Inc.">CV Productions, Inc.</a>. Its first competition, called <i>Tough Guy Contest</i> was held on March 20, 1980, <a href="/wiki/New_Kensington,_Pennsylvania" title="New Kensington, Pennsylvania">New Kensington, Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holiday_Inn" title="Holiday Inn">Holiday Inn</a>. During that year the company renamed the brand to <i>Super Fighters</i> and sanctioned ten regulated tournaments in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>. In 1983, Pennsylvania State Senate passed a bill that specifically called for: "Prohibiting Tough Guy contests or Battle of the Brawlers contests", and ended the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-NashJohn_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NashJohn-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WernerSam_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WernerSam-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, the sport was reintroduced to the United States by the Ultimate Fighting Championship (UFC).<sup id="cite_ref-Sánchez_Garcia,_R._2010_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sánchez_Garcia,_R._2010-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The sport gained international exposure and widespread publicity when jiu-jitsu fighter <a href="/wiki/Royce_Gracie" title="Royce Gracie">Royce Gracie</a> won the first Ultimate Fighting Championship tournament, submitting three challengers in a total of just five minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> sparking a revolution in martial arts.<sup id="cite_ref-peligro_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peligro-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan had its own form of mixed martial arts discipline, <a href="/wiki/Shooto" title="Shooto">Shooto</a>, which evolved from shoot wrestling in 1985, as well as the shoot wrestling derivative <a href="/wiki/Pancrase" title="Pancrase">Pancrase</a>, which was founded as a promotion in 1993. The first <a href="/wiki/Vale_Tudo_Japan" title="Vale Tudo Japan">Vale Tudo Japan</a> tournaments were held in 1994 and 1995 and were both won by <a href="/wiki/Rickson_Gracie" title="Rickson Gracie">Rickson Gracie</a>. Around the same time, International Vale Tudo competition started to develop through (World Vale Tudo Championship (WVC), <a href="/wiki/Vale_Tudo_Japan" title="Vale Tudo Japan">VTJ</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Vale_Tudo_Championship" title="International Vale Tudo Championship">IVC</a>, UVF etc.). Interest in mixed martial arts as a sport resulted in the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Pride_Fighting_Championships" title="Pride Fighting Championships">Pride Fighting Championships</a> (Pride) in 1997, where again Rickson participated and won.<sup id="cite_ref-whatispride_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whatispride-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The sport reached a new peak of popularity in North America in December 2006: a <a href="/wiki/UFC_66" title="UFC 66">rematch</a> between then <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Fighting_Championship" title="Ultimate Fighting Championship">UFC</a> light heavyweight champion <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Liddell" title="Chuck Liddell">Chuck Liddell</a> and former champion <a href="/wiki/Tito_Ortiz" title="Tito Ortiz">Tito Ortiz</a>, rivaled the <a href="/wiki/Pay-per-view" title="Pay-per-view">PPV</a> sales of some of the biggest boxing events of all time,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> and helped the UFC's 2006 PPV gross surpass that of any promotion in PPV history. In 2007, <a href="/wiki/Zuffa" title="Zuffa">Zuffa LLC</a>, the owners of the UFC MMA promotion, bought Japanese rival MMA brand <a href="/wiki/Pride_Fighting_Championships" title="Pride Fighting Championships">Pride FC</a>, merging the contracted fighters under one promotion.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Comparisons were drawn to the consolidation that occurred in other sports, such as the <a href="/wiki/AFL-NFL_Merger" class="mw-redirect" title="AFL-NFL Merger">AFL-NFL Merger</a> in <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">American football</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Origin_of_the_term_.22MMA.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_the_term_&quot;MMA&quot;">Origin of the term "MMA"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Origin of the term &quot;MMA&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first documented use of the name <i>mixed martial arts</i> was in a review of <a href="/wiki/UFC_1" title="UFC 1">UFC 1</a> by television critic <a href="/wiki/Howard_Rosenberg" title="Howard Rosenberg">Howard Rosenberg</a>, in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1993_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1993-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The term gained popularity when the website newfullcontact.com, then one of the biggest covering the sport, hosted and reprinted the article. The first use of the term by a promotion was in September 1995 by Rick Blume, president and CEO of Battlecade Extreme Fighting, just after UFC 7.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
UFC official <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Blatnick" title="Jeff Blatnick">Jeff Blatnick</a> was responsible for the Ultimate Fighting Championship officially adopting the name mixed martial arts. It was previously marketed as "Ultimate Fighting" and "No Holds Barred (NHB)", until Blatnick and <a href="/wiki/John_McCarthy_(mixed_martial_arts)" class="mw-redirect" title="John McCarthy (mixed martial arts)">John McCarthy</a> proposed the name "MMA" at the <a href="/wiki/UFC_17" title="UFC 17">UFC 17</a> rules meeting in response to increased public criticism.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The question as to who actually coined the name is still in debate.<sup id="cite_ref-KirikJenness_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KirikJenness-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first state regulated MMA event was held in Biloxi, Mississippi on August 23, 1996 with the sanctioning of IFC's Mayhem in Mississippi<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> show by the Mississippi Athletic Commission under William Lyons. The rules used were an adaptation of the kickboxing rules already accepted by most state athletic commissions. These modified kickboxing rules allowed for take downs and ground fighting and did away with rounds, although they did allow for fighters to be stood up by the referee and restarted if there was no action on the ground. These rules were the first in modern MMA to define fouls, fighting surfaces and the use of the cage.
</p><p>In March 1997, the Iowa Athletic Commission officially sanctioned <a href="/wiki/Battlecade_Extreme_Fighting" title="Battlecade Extreme Fighting">Battlecade Extreme Fighting</a> under a modified form of its existing rules for <a href="/wiki/Shootfighting" title="Shootfighting">Shootfighting</a>. These rules created the three 5 minute round, one-minute break format, and mandated shootfighting gloves, as well as weight classes, for the first time. Illegal blows were listed as groin strikes, head butting, biting, eye gouging, hair pulling, striking an opponent with an elbow while the opponent is on the mat, kidney strikes, and striking the back of the head with closed fist. Holding onto the ring or cage for any reason was defined as a foul.<sup id="cite_ref-EXTREME_FIGHTING_4_RULES_MEETING_GOES_ON_AS_PLANNED_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EXTREME_FIGHTING_4_RULES_MEETING_GOES_ON_AS_PLANNED-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Iowa_Professional_Shoot_Fighting_Regulations_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iowa_Professional_Shoot_Fighting_Regulations-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> While there are minor differences between these and the final Unified Rules, notably regarding elbow strikes, the Iowa rules allowed mixed martial arts promoters to conduct essentially modern events legally, anywhere in the state. On March 28, 1997, Extreme Fighting 4 was held under these rules, making it the first show conducted under a version of the modern rules.
</p><p>In April 2000, the <a href="/wiki/California_State_Athletic_Commission" title="California State Athletic Commission">California State Athletic Commission</a> voted unanimously in favor of regulations that later became the foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Rules_of_Mixed_Martial_Arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Unified Rules of Mixed Martial Arts">Unified Rules of Mixed Martial Arts</a>. However, when the legislation was sent to the California capital in Sacramento for review, it was determined that the sport fell outside the jurisdiction of the CSAC, rendering the vote meaningless.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On September 30, 2000, the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_State_Athletic_Control_Board" title="New Jersey State Athletic Control Board">New Jersey State Athletic Control Board</a> (NJSACB) began allowing mixed martial arts promoters to conduct events in New Jersey. The first event was an IFC event titled Battleground 2000 held in Atlantic City. The intent was to allow the NJSACB to observe actual events and gather information to establish a comprehensive set of rules to regulate the sport effectively.<sup id="cite_ref-njacb_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-njacb-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On April 3, 2001, the NJSACB held a meeting to discuss the regulation of mixed martial arts events. This meeting attempted to unify the myriad rules and regulations which had been utilized by the different mixed martial arts organizations. At this meeting, the proposed uniform rules were agreed upon by the NJSACB, several other regulatory bodies, numerous promoters of mixed martial arts events and other interested parties in attendance. At the conclusion of the meeting, all parties in attendance were able to agree upon a uniform set of rules to govern the sport of mixed martial arts.<sup id="cite_ref-njacb_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-njacb-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rules adopted by the NJSACB have become the de facto standard set of rules for professional mixed martial arts across North America. On July 30, 2009, a motion was made at the annual meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Boxing_Commissions" title="Association of Boxing Commissions">Association of Boxing Commissions</a> to adopt these rules as the "Unified Rules of Mixed Martial Arts". The motion passed unanimously.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2005 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> began to sanction mixed martial arts with the first annual Army Combatives Championships held by the <a href="/wiki/US_Army_Combatives_School" class="mw-redirect" title="US Army Combatives School">US Army Combatives School</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> formally decriminalized mixed martial arts with a vote on Bill S-209 on June 5, 2013. The bill allows for provinces to have the power to create athletic commissions to regulate and sanction professional mixed martial arts bouts.<sup id="cite_ref-CANL1_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CANL1-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="MMA_organizations">MMA organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: MMA organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Promotions">Promotions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Promotions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Mixed_martial_arts#MMA_fighter_ranking" title="Mixed martial arts">MMA portal</a> <i>Tapology.com</i> listings, hundreds of MMA promotions around the world produce MMA events.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the UFC came to prominence in <a href="/wiki/Mainstream_media" title="Mainstream media">mainstream media</a> in 2006, and with their 2007 merger with Pride FC and purchases of <a href="/wiki/World_Extreme_Cagefighting" title="World Extreme Cagefighting">WEC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strikeforce_(mixed_martial_arts)" title="Strikeforce (mixed martial arts)">Strikeforce</a>, no companies have presented significant competition,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and the UFC is regarded as having most of the <a href="/wiki/Mixed_martial_arts#MMA_fighter_ranking" title="Mixed martial arts">top ranked</a> talent.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Fighters usually get contracts in the UFC after competing successfully in other MMA promotions from around the world.
</p><p>Some MMA promotions tend to exist more to build up prospects while others have a good mix of prospects and veterans.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Some exist only to be feeder leagues to the bigger promotions (e.g. <i>LFA</i>, <i>CWFC</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> others exist to try to be the best in the world (e.g. <i>ACB</i>, <i>ONE Championship</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Some promotions only do four shows a year while others are doing them monthly.
</p><p>According to <i>ScorecardMMA.com</i>, one of the world's top statistical rankings services for MMA,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> and the only one currently ranking MMA promotions worldwide statistically; "To rank MMA Promotions we use an index that we calculate weekly- <i>Promotion Score</i>.Promo Score calculation is based on the strength of Events and Fighters that Promotion has at that time. Promo Score has three main components:
</p>
<ul><li>Event Index - based on the quality of events in the last quarter and in the last year.</li>
<li>Owned Fighter Index - calculated based on the quality of fighters that the promotion currently owns (contracted, or used recently).</li>
<li>Utilized Fighters Index - fighters that the promotion has used in the last four months and in the last three quarters.</li></ul>
<p>As of 26 May 2018 the top 15 MMA promotions (out of 41 ranked) in the world are:<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>1. <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Fighting_Championship" title="Ultimate Fighting Championship">Ultimate Fighting Championship (UFC)</a>. Based out of Las Vegas, United States. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Fox_Sports" title="Fox Sports">Fox Sports</a> (from 2019 will be on <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>) and on other networks around the world.</li>
<li>2. <a href="/wiki/Bellator_MMA" title="Bellator MMA">Bellator MMA</a>. Based out of Newport Beach, California, United States. Broadcasts their fights locally on the <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Network" title="Paramount Network">Paramount Network</a> and other networks around the world.</li>
<li>3. <a href="/wiki/Absolute_Championship_Berkut" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute Championship Berkut">Absolute Championship Berkut</a> (ACB). Based out of <a href="/wiki/Grozny" title="Grozny">Grozny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Match_TV" title="Match TV">Match TV</a> and streaming on <a href="/wiki/YouTube" title="YouTube">YouTube</a> (with Russian commentary) and <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> (with English commentary).</li>
<li>4. <a href="/wiki/Fight_Nights_Global" title="Fight Nights Global">Fight Nights Global</a> (FNG). Based out of <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, Russia. Broadcasts their fights locally on <i>Match TV</i>, and streaming on <a href="/wiki/VKontakte" class="mw-redirect" title="VKontakte">VKontakte</a>.</li>
<li>5. <a href="/wiki/ONE_Championship" title="ONE Championship">ONE Championship</a>. Based out of Kallang, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>. Broadcasts their fights regionally on <a href="/wiki/Fox_Sports_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Fox Sports Asia">Fox Sports Asia</a> and streaming on their <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">Mobile app</a> (without <a href="/wiki/Geo-blocking" title="Geo-blocking">Geo-blocking</a>).</li>
<li>6. <a href="/wiki/Rizin_Fighting_Federation" title="Rizin Fighting Federation">Rizin Fighting Federation</a> (Rizin FF). Based out of Tokyo, Japan. Broadcast their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuji Television">Fuji Television</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Pay-per-view" title="Pay-per-view">Pay-per-view</a> on <a href="/wiki/SKY_PerfecTV!" title="SKY PerfecTV!">SKY PerfecTV!</a> (Japan only) and <i>Fite.tv</i> (internationally).</li>
<li>7. <a href="/wiki/Invicta_FC" class="mw-redirect" title="Invicta FC">Invicta FC</a> (all female MMA). Based out of Enka, North Carolina, United States. Broadcasts their fights on <a href="/wiki/UFC_Fight_Pass" title="UFC Fight Pass">UFC Fight Pass</a>.</li>
<li>8. <a href="/wiki/Pancrase" title="Pancrase">Pancrase</a>. Based out of Tokyo, Japan. Broadcast their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_MX" title="Tokyo MX">Tokyo MX</a> and streaming on <a href="/wiki/AbemaTV" title="AbemaTV">AbemaTV</a> (with Japanese commentary) and on UFC Fight Pass (with English commentary).</li>
<li>9. <a href="/wiki/M-1_Global" title="M-1 Global">M-1 Global</a>. Based out of <a href="/wiki/St._Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Petersburg">St. Petersburg</a>, Russia. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Russia-2" title="Russia-2">Russia-2</a> and streaming on their mobile app (with English commentary).</li>
<li>10. <a href="/wiki/Legacy_Fighting_Alliance" title="Legacy Fighting Alliance">Legacy Fighting Alliance</a> (LFA). Based out of Houston, Texas, USA. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/AXS_TV_Fights" title="AXS TV Fights">AXS TV Fights</a>.</li>
<li>11. World Fighting Championship Akhmat (WFCA). Based out of Grozny, Russia. Broadcasts their fights locally on <i>Grozny TV</i> (also streaming on <i>Grozny.tv</i>).</li>
<li>12. <a href="/wiki/Konfrontacja_Sztuk_Walki" title="Konfrontacja Sztuk Walki">Konfrontacja Sztuk Walki</a> (KSW). Based out of: Warsaw, <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/Polsat_Sport" title="Polsat Sport">Polsat Sport</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Fight_Network" title="Fight Network">Fight Network</a> internationally.</li>
<li>13. <a href="/wiki/Deep_(mixed_martial_arts)" title="Deep (mixed martial arts)">Deep</a>. Based out of <a href="/wiki/Nagoya" title="Nagoya">Nagoya</a>, Japan. Broadcast their fights locally on <i>SKY PerfecTV!</i> (Fighting TV Samurai channel) and streaming on AbemaTV.</li>
<li>14. <a href="/wiki/Road_Fighting_Championship" title="Road Fighting Championship">Road Fighting Championship</a> (Road FC). Based out of <a href="/wiki/Wonju" title="Wonju">Wonju</a>, South Korea. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/MBC_Sports%2B" title="MBC Sports+">MBC Sports+</a> and streaming on <a href="/wiki/AfreecaTV" title="AfreecaTV">afreecaTV</a> (with Korean commentary) and <i>YouTube</i> (with English commentary).</li>
<li>15. <a href="/wiki/Cage_Warriors" title="Cage Warriors">Cage Warriors</a> (CWFC). Based out of London, England, UK. Broadcasts their fights locally on <a href="/wiki/BT_Sport" title="BT Sport">BT Sport</a> and on UFC Fight Pass.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gyms">Gyms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gyms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_professional_MMA_training_camps" title="List of professional MMA training camps">List of professional MMA training camps</a></div>
<p>There are hundreds of MMA training facilities throughout the world.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>These are the current top MMA Gyms in the world based on how many top ranked UFC fighters they currently train. There are 160 fighters, who train at over 80 different gyms, in the top 15 of the UFC's 10 divisions. The rankings are based on a system where a champion earns their gym 25 points, A #1 contender is 15, a #2 contender 10 and then a decrease in ranking equaling a decrease of 1 point until the ranking reaches #10. From there, rankings 11-15 are each worth 1 point. (updated July 2015):<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>1. <a href="/wiki/Nova_Uni%C3%A3o_(mixed_martial_arts)" title="Nova União (mixed martial arts)">Nova União</a> located in Rio de Janeiro, Brazil</li>
<li>2. <a href="/wiki/Greg_Jackson_(MMA_trainer)" title="Greg Jackson (MMA trainer)">Jackson-Winkeljohn MMA</a> located in Albuquerque, New Mexico</li>
<li>3. <a href="/wiki/American_Kickboxing_Academy" title="American Kickboxing Academy">American Kickboxing Academy</a> (AKA) located in San Jose, California.</li>
<li>4. <a href="/wiki/Team_Alpha_Male" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Alpha Male">Team Alpha Male</a> located in Sacramento, California.</li>
<li>5. <a href="/wiki/American_Top_Team" title="American Top Team">American Top Team</a> (ATT) located in Coconut Creek, Florida.</li>
<li>6. <a href="/wiki/Kings_MMA" title="Kings MMA">Kings MMA</a> located in Huntington Beach, California.</li>
<li>7. <a href="/wiki/Blackzilians" title="Blackzilians">Blackzilians</a> located in Boca Raton, Florida.</li>
<li>8. <a href="/wiki/Matt_Serra#Personal_life" title="Matt Serra">Serra-Longo</a> located in Long Island, New York.</li>
<li>9. Glendale Fighting Club located in Glendale, California.</li>
<li>10. <a href="/wiki/Black_House_(MMA)" title="Black House (MMA)">Black House</a> (Team Nogueira) based out of Rio de Janeiro, Brazil.</li>
<li>11. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.TeixeiraMMAandFitness.com/">Teixeira MMA &amp; Fitness</a> (Teixeira MMA) located in Bethel, Connecticut.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mixed_martial_arts&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Web data traffic ranking leader <a href="/wiki/Alexa_Internet" title="Alexa Internet">Alexa Internet</a> lists 40 online media outlets under its "MMA news and media" website category. As of November 13, 2017, the top 10 most popular websites covering the sport are:<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>1. <a href="/wiki/Sherdog" title="Sherdog">Sherdog</a>.com</li>
<li>2. MMAFighting.com (<a href="/wiki/SB_Nation" title="SB Nation">SB Nation</a>)</li>
<li>3. <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Fighting_Championship" title="Ultimate Fighting Championship">UFC</a>.com</li>
